Nikah — Marriage

Remission of Mahr and Confirmation by Khalwa
Commentary on remission of mahr and khalwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a wife's remission of mahr and explain how khalwa (valid seclusion) confirms the full mahr.

Core Idea
A wife's voluntary remission of the mahr is valid and binding if accepted by the husband; khalwa (valid seclusion) triggers the full mahr like intercourse.

This lesson covers two important aspects: the wife's right to remit the mahr, and the role of khalwa in confirming the mahr. We will see the proofs from Quran and hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of remission (hatt)
The wife remits the mahr (or part of it) after the contract, releasing the husband's liability.
2
State the ruling and proof
It is valid and the husband is released from that amount; proof is Quran 4:4. However, it is revocable if the husband rejects.
3
Understand khalwa (seclusion)
Valid seclusion without barriers to intercourse confirms the full mahr, just like actual intercourse.
4
Identify the proof for khalwa
From Quran 4:20-21, where 'afda' (going in unto) is interpreted as khalwa, and a hadith (weak but used).
5
Note the effect
Once khalwa occurs, the full mahr becomes wajib, and it is not affected by subsequent divorce without consummation.

Source Text

قوله : زيد على المهر: أي المسمى عن العقد، ولو كان بلا شهود أو بعد هبة المهر والإبراء منه سواء كان من جنس المهر أو غير جنسه ، وسواء كانت الزيادة من الزوج أو وليه، فقد صرحوا بأن الأب والجد لو زوج ابنَه ثم زاد في المهر صح. كذا في «النهر» و«البحر»(۱). ولا يشترط لفظ : الزيادة، بل تصح بلفظها ، وبقوله : راجعتك بكذا إن قبلت، كذا في «أنفع الوسائل»، وذكر في «الخانية»: إنه لو وهبته مهرها، ثم أقر بكذا وقبلت صح، ويحمل على الزيادة. [٢] قوله : يجب ؛ أي على الزوج لكن بشرط قبول الزوجة في المجلس أو ولي الصغيرة، ومعرفته قدر الزيادة، وبقاء الزوجية حين الزيادة. وكذا في «النهر»، وفي وجوب الزيادة خلاف زفر ، ولا وجه له، ويدلُّ على قولنا قوله الا : فيما تراضيتُم بِهِ مِنْ بَعْدِ الْفَرِيضَةِ (٢). His statement: 'Increased upon the mahr' means the named (mahr) from the contract, even if it was without witnesses or after gifting the mahr and releasing from it, whether the increase is from the same type as the mahr or different, and whether the increase is from the husband or his guardian. They explicitly stated that if the father or grandfather married off his son and then increased the mahr, it is valid. Likewise in 'al-Nahr' and 'al-Bahr' (1). The wording 'increase' is not required; rather it is valid with its wording, and with his statement: 'I return to you for such-and-such if you accept,' as in 'Anfa' al-Wasa'il'. And it is mentioned in 'al-Khaniyya': If she gifted him her mahr, then he acknowledged something and she accepted, it is valid, and it is interpreted as an increase. [2] His statement: 'It is obligatory' means upon the husband, but with the condition of the wife's acceptance in the session or the guardian of the minor, and his knowledge of the amount of the increase, and the continuation of the marriage at the time of the increase. Likewise in 'al-Nahr'. And there is a disagreement with Zufar regarding the obligation of the increase, and it has no basis. And what indicates our statement is His saying: 'except regarding what you mutually agree upon after the prescribed obligation' (2). [3] His statement: 'with divorce' means if he divorces before consummation, only half of the original is obligatory and the increase is not halved. And there is a disagreement of Abu Yusuf in his first statement. Our proof is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding the surah of divorce before consummation: 'then you give half of what you prescribed' (3). For the apparent meaning is that the prescribed (mahr) is interpreted as the usual prescribed, which is the prescribed at the contract, so what is increased after it is not halved. (3) [4] His statement: 'her remission' - with fatha and tashdid of ta - meaning cancellation: i.e., if the woman remits the mahr from the husband after the contract, that is valid, and that amount remitted falls off, even if it was the entire mahr from his liability; due to His saying: 'But if they give up willingly to you anything of it, then consume it with enjoyment and pleasure' (4). And the remission and likewise the release are revocable by the husband's rejection, as in 'al-Qunniya' and 'al-Bahr'. (1) 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' (3:160). (2) Women: from verse 24. (3) al-Baqara: from verse 237. (4) Women: from verse 4. And seclusion without a barrier to intercourse. 'Umdat al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya. [1] His statement: 'And it was not mentioned' means the object of 'remission' is not mentioned to indicate generality, as in the saying: 'So-and-so gives and withholds' so it indicates the remission of all of the mahr or part of it, and the increase in the case where he increased upon the mahr. And seclusion without a barrier to intercourse. [1] His statement: 'And it was not mentioned' - in the passive voice, so what follows is a substitute for the doer, or in the active voice, so its agent is the pronoun referring to the author, and what follows is the object. [2] His statement: 'to indicate generality' - for the omission of the object may be for the purpose of generalization and absoluteness as in their praise of generous, intelligent people: 'So-and-so gives and withholds' i.e., he gives every beneficial thing and withholds every harmful thing, for if he mentioned the object in such examples it would indicate specification. [3] His statement: 'and the increase' i.e., the remission of the increase that the husband added after the contract. [4] His statement: 'and seclusion' - a beginning in explaining what confirms the mahr besides intercourse. The basis in this chapter is His saying: 'And if you wish to substitute one wife in place of another and you have given one of them a great amount, do not take anything from it' until He said: 'And how can you take it while you have gone in unto each other?' (1) For 'going in' is interpreted as seclusion. (2) And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever uncovered a woman and looked at her, then the bridal gift becomes obligatory, whether he entered upon her or did not enter' - narrated by al-Daraqutni with a weak chain, and by Abu Dawud in his 'Mursalat' with a chain whose narrators are trustworthy.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Al-Wiqayah regarding the rulings of the mahr (dower) in the chapter of marriage. The commentator explains the matn line by line. The matn states: 'Whatever is increased upon the mahr is obligatory, and it falls off by divorce before intercourse, and her remission of it is valid.' The commentary first defines 'increase upon the mahr' as an addition to the named mahr from the contract, regardless of whether witnesses were present, or after the wife has gifted the mahr or released the husband from it. The increase can be from the same type of mahr or a different type, and the person making the increase can be the husband or his guardian (such as the father or grandfather marrying off his son). The increase is valid even without using the word 'increase'; it can be expressed as 'I return to you for such-and-such if you accept.' The condition for the increase to be obligatory on the husband is the wife's acceptance in the same session (or the guardian's acceptance if she is a minor), the husband's knowledge of the amount, and the continuation of the marriage at the time of the increase. There is a disagreement with Zufar who denied the obligation of such an increase, but the commentator dismisses it as baseless and supports the Hanafi position with the verse from Surah al-Nisa' (4:24): 'except regarding what you mutually agree upon after the prescribed obligation.' The commentary then addresses the effect of divorce before consummation: the original mahr (the one fixed at the contract) is halved, but the increase added later is not halved—it falls off entirely. This is based on the Prophet's interpretation of the verse in Surah al-Baqarah (2:237) regarding divorce before consummation: 'then give half of what you prescribed.' The 'prescribed' is interpreted as the mahr fixed at the contract, so the increase is not subject to halving. Abu Yusuf initially differed but the Hanafi position is established. Next, the commentary explains the wife's remission (hatt) of the mahr: if she voluntarily remits all or part of the mahr after the contract, it is valid and the husband is released from that amount. The proof is the verse from Surah al-Nisa' (4:4): 'But if they give up willingly to you anything of it, then consume it with enjoyment and pleasure.' However, the remission is revocable if the husband rejects it, as mentioned in the sources. Finally, the commentary introduces the concept of khalwa (seclusion) as a factor that confirms the full mahr, even without intercourse. The basis is the verse in Surah al-Nisa' (4:20-21) where 'going in unto each other' is interpreted as seclusion. Additionally, a hadith narrated by al-Daraqutni and Abu Dawud states that if a man uncovers a woman (i.e., removes her head covering) and looks at her, the full bridal gift becomes obligatory, whether or not he actually entered upon her. The chain is considered weak but is supported by other narrations.

Key Terms
الحط (al-ḥaṭṭ): The remission or cancellation of the mahr (or part of it) by the wife in favor of the husband after the contract.
الخلوة (al-khalwah): A valid seclusion between husband and wife without any legal impediment to intercourse, which triggers the obligation of the full mahr just like actual intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the effect of a wife's remission of the mahr if the husband accepts?
A) The husband is released from the mahr
B) The remission is void
C) The wife can revoke later
D) Half the mahr is still due

Key Takeaway
A wife's remission of mahr is valid and binding upon husband's acceptance; khalwa (valid seclusion) confirms the full mahr like intercourse.

Khalwah and Its Effect on the Mahr
Explaining the conditions for khalwah to confirm the entire mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi conditions for a valid khalwah that confirms the full mahr and distinguish it from invalid seclusion.

Core Idea
A valid khalwah without impediment confirms the full mahr, just like actual intercourse.

In this lesson, we dive into a key ruling in Hanafi marriage law: when does seclusion (khalwah) make the entire mahr obligatory? The commentator expands on the conditions and proofs, showing that the ruling is not arbitrary but grounded in Quran, hadith, and logical reasoning. Understanding this will help you assess scenarios where a marriage ends before consummation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its evidence
The passage states that a valid khalwah confirms the full mahr. Locate the primary evidence: the Quranic verse (An-Nisa 4:20-21) where 'ifda' is interpreted as khalwah, and the hadith (though weak) and Companion practices.
Note that the Shafi'i school disagrees with this ruling.
2
List the conditions for a valid khalwah
The text specifies that khalwah must be without any impediment (mani') to intercourse. Identify the three categories: physical, legal, natural. Each category has examples.
The presence of a third person is considered a natural impediment by some.
3
Analyze the reasoning (wajh)
The commentator explains that khalwah typically leads to intercourse, and since intercourse is hidden, the law treats the cause as the effect, analogous to sleep and wudu nullification. Work through this qiyas.
This is a form of istihsan? Actually it is qiyas with a clear illa.
4
Apply the ruling to scenarios
Consider different impediments: illness (physical), Ramadan fast (legal), menstruation (natural). Determine whether each would prevent khalwah from confirming the full mahr.
If impediment exists, only half the mahr is due upon separation before consummation.
5
Recognize the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school does not accept khalwah as a substitute for actual intercourse. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: in a Shafi'i marriage, if the husband divorces before consummation, only half the mahr is due even if there was seclusion.
This difference matters in fatwa when couples move between madhhabs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر "مفعول الحطّ ليدل على العموم، كما في قوله : فلانٌ يُعطي ويمنع، فيدلُّ على حطّ كل المهر وبعضه، والزيادة" في صورة زاد على المهر عنه. وخلوة بلا مانع وطء
[١] قوله : ولم يُذكر : بصيغة المجهول فما بعده نائب مناب فاعله، أو بصيغة المعلوم ففاعله الضمير الراجع إلى المصنف له، وما بعده مفعول.
[٢] قوله : ليدل على العموم ؛ فإن حذف المفعول قد يكون لإفادة التعميم والإطلاق كما في قولهم في مدح الأسخياء العقلاء : فلان يعطي ويمنع : أي يعطي كلَّ شيء نافع ويمنع كل ضار، فإنّه لو ذكر المفعول في أمثال هذا لإفادة التخصيص.
[۳] قوله : والزيادة ؛ أي حط الزيادة التي زادها الزوج بعد العقد.
[٤] قوله : وخلوة ؛ شروع في بيان ما يؤكد المهر غير الوطء، والأصل في هذا الباب قوله : وَإِن أَردتُمُ اسْتِبْدَالَ زَوْجٍ مَكَانَ زَوج وَمَاتَيْتُمْ إِحْدَتْهُنَّ قِنطَارًا فَلَا تَأْخُذُوا مِنْهُ شَيْئًا ) إلى أن قال : وَكَيْفَ تَأْخُذُونَهُ، وَقَدْ أَفْضَى بَعْضُكُمْ إِلَى بعض ) ، فإن الإفضاء مفسر بالخلوة.
وقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : «من كشف خمر امرأة ونظر إليها فقد وجب الصداق دخل بها أو لم يدخل) ، أخرجه الدَّارَ قُطْنِيُّ بسند ضعيف ، وأبو داود في «مراسيله» بإسناد رجاله ثقات.
وخلوة بلا مانع وطء حساً، أو شرعاً، أو طبعاً : كمرض يمنع الوطء، وصوم رمضان، وإحرام بفرض، أو نفل، وحيض ونفاس (وخلوة بلا مانع وطء حساً، أو شرعاً ، أو طبعاً : كمرض يمنع الوطء) : هذا نظير المانع " الحسي.
(وصوم رمضان وإحرام بفرض أو نفل) : هذا نظير المانع الشرعي. (وحيض ونفاس) : هذا نظير المانع الطبعي
وعن عمر بن الخطاب : «إذا رُخَيت الستور فقد وجب الصداق»(۱). أخرجه مالك في «الموطأ» والبيهقي، وعبد الرزاق، ومثلُهُ أخرجَهُ الدَّارَقُطِنِيُّ عن علي وأبو عبيد في «كتاب النكاح» عن الخلفاء الأربعة.
والوجه فيه أن الخلوة مفضية غالباً إلى الوطء، وحقيقة الوطء أمر يخفى غالباً، فأقيم السبب المفضي إليه مقامه ، كما أقيم النوم مقام الحدث في نقض الوضوء ، مع أن م أنها إذا خَلَتْ به سَلَّمَت المبدل ، حيث رَفَعَت الموانع وأنت ما في وسعها، فيجب البدلُ ، ومن هاهنا اشترط في الخلوة المؤكدة عدم المانع من الوطء؛ لأن الخلوة مع المانع ليست بمفضية إلى الوطء فلا تقام مقامه.
[1]قوله : حساً ؛ أي سواء كان المانع من الوطء حسيّاً أو شرعياً، أو طبعياً : أي أمراً يمنعه من الوطء بمقتضى الحس، أو الشرع، أو الطبع.
[٢) قوله : يمنع الوطء ; صفةٌ لمرض ، وقيَّد به ؛ لأنه ليس كل مرض مانعاً من الوطء، وأطلقه فشمل مرض الزوج والزوجة ، ومنه أن تكون المرأة ذات رَتَق ، وهو - بفتحتين - : تلاحم موضع الوطء، أو تكون ذات قرن : وهو – بالفتح وبفتحتين – : مانع يمنع من سلوك الذكر في الفرج كالعظم والغدة الغليظة. ومن المانع الحسي: صغر الزوج أو الزوجة بحيث لا يقدر على الجماع، ومنه وجود ثالث معهما ، ومنهم : من جعله مثالاً للطبع.
(۳) قوله : هذا نظير المانع ; الطبع فإن الدم بموضع الوطء مما يتنفّر عن الوطء عنده الطبع الإنساني السليم.
And he did not mention the object of 'al-hatt' to indicate generality, as in his saying: 'So-and-so gives and withholds,' so it indicates the reduction of all the mahr and part of it, and the increase in the case where he increased beyond the mahr. And a seclusion without an impediment to intercourse.
[1] His saying: 'And it was not mentioned': in the passive voice, so what follows is a substitute for the agent, or in the active voice, so its agent is the pronoun referring back to the author, and what follows is the object.
[2] His saying: 'to indicate generality': for the omission of the object may be to convey generality and absoluteness, as in their saying in praise of generous and intelligent people: 'So-and-so gives and withholds,' i.e., he gives every beneficial thing and withholds every harmful thing, for if he mentioned the object in such examples, it would convey specification.
[3] His saying: 'and the increase': i.e., the reduction of the increase that the husband added after the contract.
[4] His saying: 'and seclusion': a commencement of explaining what confirms the mahr other than intercourse. The basis in this chapter is His saying: 'And if you wish to replace a wife with another and you have given one of them a great amount, do not take anything from it,' until He said: 'And how could you take it while you have come unto one another?' For 'ifda'' is interpreted as seclusion.
And the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever uncovers the head covering of a woman and looks at her, the dower becomes obligatory, whether he has intercourse with her or not.' Ad-Daraqutni narrated it with a weak chain, and Abu Dawud in his Marasil with a chain whose narrators are trustworthy.
And a seclusion without an impediment to intercourse, physically, legally, or naturally: like an illness that prevents intercourse, Ramadan fast, ihram for an obligatory or supererogatory pilgrimage, menstruation and postpartum bleeding. (And a seclusion without an impediment to intercourse physically, legally, or naturally: like an illness that prevents intercourse): this is an example of a physical impediment.
(And Ramadan fast and ihram for an obligatory or supererogatory pilgrimage): this is an example of a legal impediment. (And menstruation and postpartum bleeding): this is an example of a natural impediment.
And from Umar ibn al-Khattab: 'If the curtains have been let down, the dower becomes obligatory.' Malik narrated it in al-Muwatta', al-Bayhaqi, and Abd ar-Razzaq, and similarly Ad-Daraqutni narrated it from Ali, and Abu Ubayd in the Book of Marriage from the four caliphs.
The reasoning is that seclusion usually leads to intercourse, and the reality of intercourse is something that is usually hidden, so the cause that leads to it is treated as its substitute, just as sleep is treated as a substitute for the occurrence of an invalidator in the nullification of wudu, even though it (seclusion) is that when she is alone with him, she submits the substitute, for she removes the impediments to the best of her ability, so the substitute is obligatory. And from this, it is required in the affirming seclusion that there be no impediment to intercourse, because seclusion with an impediment is not conducive to intercourse, so it is not treated as its substitute.
[1] His saying: 'physically': meaning whether the impediment to intercourse is physical, legal, or natural: i.e., something that prevents intercourse by virtue of the senses, law, or nature.
[2] His saying: 'that prevents intercourse': an adjective for 'illness', and it is qualified by it, because not every illness prevents intercourse, and it is absolute so it includes illness of the husband and the wife. Among them is that the woman has ratq, which (with two fat'has) is the fusion of the place of intercourse, or she has qarn, which (with a fat'h and two fat'has) is an impediment that prevents the entry of the male organ into the vagina, like bone or a thick gland. Among the physical impediments is the smallness of the husband or wife such that they cannot engage in intercourse, and among them is the presence of a third person with them, and some have made it an example of the natural.
[3] His saying: 'this is an example of an impediment': the natural, for blood in the place of intercourse is something from which the sound human nature recoils from intercourse.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the ruling that a valid khalwah (unlawful seclusion) confirms the entire mahr, just as actual intercourse does. The commentator first explains why the author did not mention the object of 'hatt' (reduction) — it is omitted to indicate generality, meaning that the mahr is confirmed in full or in part depending on the situation, and if the husband increased the mahr, the reduction applies to the increase as well. Then the commentator begins to explain the ruling on khalwah. The primary evidence is the Quranic verse (An-Nisa 4:20-21) where 'ifda' (coming to one another) is interpreted as khalwah, indicating that seclusion after a valid marriage contract makes the entire mahr obligatory. A hadith from the Prophet (ﷺ) is also cited, but it is weak; however, the practice of the Companions (such as the four caliphs) supports the ruling. The key condition for a khalwah to be effective is that there must be no impediment to intercourse. Impediments are categorized into three types: physical (hissi), legal (shar'i), and natural (tab'i). Physical impediments include illness that prevents intercourse, the woman having ratq (fusion) or qarn (bony obstruction), or the smallness of the spouses. Legal impediments include fasting in Ramadan, being in the state of ihram (pilgrimage consecration), and the wife being in her menstrual or postpartum period, all of which legally prohibit intercourse. Natural impediments include the presence of a third person or blood in the place of intercourse, which naturally repels the desire. The reasoning (wajh) is that khalwah typically leads to intercourse, and since actual intercourse is concealed, the law treats the cause (seclusion) as a substitute for the effect (intercourse), similar to how sleep is treated as a substitute for the occurrence of an invalidator in wudu. The woman, by secluding with the husband, submits to him and removes impediments within her capacity, so the mahr becomes obligatory. Therefore, the ruling is specific to a seclusion where there is no impediment to intercourse.

Key Terms
خلوة (khalwah): Unlawful seclusion of a married couple in a place where intercourse is possible, with no third person present who can see them.
مانع (mani'): An impediment that legally or actually prevents sexual intercourse between the spouses.
صداق (sadaq): The dower (mahr) that becomes obligatory upon the husband due to the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered a legal (shar‘i) impediment to intercourse that would prevent khalwah from confirming the full mahr?
A) Illness that prevents intercourse
B) Ramadan fast
C) Ihram for obligatory Hajj
D) Menstruation

Key Takeaway
A valid khalwah (seclusion) after a valid marriage contract confirms the full mahr, provided there is no physical, legal, or natural impediment to intercourse.

Nikah — Marriage

Valid Seclusion and Its Effect on Mahr
Commentary on khiwat sahihah obligating full mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid seclusion (khalwah sahihah) and apply the Hanafi ruling that it necessitates full mahr.

Core Idea
Valid seclusion obligates full mahr because it is the dominant means leading to intercourse, just as sleep breaks wudu.

This passage from the Sharh al-Wiqayah explains a key Hanafi rule: when a husband and wife are lawfully alone in a place suitable for intercourse without any barrier, the full mahr becomes due even if no intercourse occurs. The commentator grounds this in the famous athar of Umar (رضي الله عنه) and rational analogy (qiyas). Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly applying the rule in nikah and divorce cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the legal effect of valid seclusion: full mahr becomes wajib (obligatory) even without intercourse.
This is the central ruling from the matn: 'وخلوة بلا مانع... تؤكده' — seclusion without barrier affirms the mahr.
2
Examine the Proof (Dalil)
Identify the athar of Umar ibn al-Khattab: 'إذا رُخَيت الستور فقد وجب الصداق' and note it is reported from multiple caliphs.
This is a hadith of zanni thubut, but the Hanafis take it as a strong indicator.
3
Analyze the Rationale (Wajh)
Seclusion is the predominant means to intercourse, which is private; the cause (seclusion) is given the status of the effect (intercourse), analogous to sleep and wudu.
This is qiyas jaliy: the means is treated as the end when the end is hidden.
4
List the Conditions for Validity
Identify the shurut: both spouses in one place suitable for intercourse, no rational third person, husband knows she is his wife, and absence of any barrier (physical, legal, natural).
Barriers are classified into three types (see commentary).
5
Test Edge Cases
Apply the rule to cases like impotence (majbub, 'innin) or menstruation — the text holds that these do not invalidate seclusion's effect on mahr.
Menstruation is a natural barrier, not a legal one, so seclusion remains valid.

وخلوة بلا مانع وطء حساً، أو شرعاً ، أو طبعاً : كمرض يمنع الوطء، وصوم رمضان، وإحرام بفرض، أو نفل، وحيض ونفاس (وخلوة بلا مانع وطء حساً، أو شرعاً ، أو طبعاً : كمرض يمنع الوطء") : هذا نظير المانع " الحسي. (وصوم رمضان وإحرام بفرض أو نفل) : هذا نظير المانع الشرعي. (وحيض ونفاس) : هذا نظير المانع الطبعي
وعن عمر بن الخطاب : «إذا رُخَيت الستور فقد وجب الصداق»(۱). أخرجه مالك في «الموطأ» والبيهقي، وعبد الرزاق، ومثلُهُ أخرجَهُ الدَّارَقُطِنِيُّ عن علي وأبو عبيد في «كتاب النكاح» عن الخلفاء الأربعة.
والوجه فيه أن الخلوة مفضية غالباً إلى الوطء، وحقيقة الوطء أمر يخفى غالباً، فأقيم السبب المفضي إليه مقامه ، كما أقيم النوم مقام الحدث في نقض الوضوء ، مع أن م أنها إذا خَلَتْ به سَلَّمَت المبدل ، حيث رَفَعَت الموانع وأنت ما في وسعها، فيجب البدلُ ، ومن هاهنا اشترط في الخلوة المؤكدة عدم المانع من الوطء؛ لأن الخلوة مع المانع ليست بمفضية إلى الوطء فلا تقام مقامه. [٢) قوله : يمنع الوطء ؛ صفةٌ لمرض ، وقيَّد به ؛ لأنه ليس كل مرض مانعاً من الوطء، وأطلقه فشمل مرض الزوج والزوجة ، ومنه أن تكون المرأة ذات رَتَق ، وهو - بفتحتين - : تلاحم موضع الوطء، أو تكون ذات قرن : وهو – بالفتح وبفتحتين – : مانع يمنع من سلوك الذكر في الفرج كالعظم والغدة الغليظة. ومن المانع الحسي: صغر الزوج أو الزوجة بحيث لا يقدر على الجماع، ومنه وجود ثالث معهما ، ومنهم : من جعله مثالاً للطبع. (۳) قوله : هذا نظير المانع ؛ الطبع فإن الدم بموضع الوطء مما يتنفّر عن الوطء عنده الطبع الإنساني السليم. 
تؤكده ، كخلوةِ مَجْبُوب، أو عِنِّين، أو خَصِيّ
ولا يضر أن يكون المانع الشرعي موجوداً فيها.
تؤكده) : أي تؤكد المهر، فخلوة : مبتدأ، وتؤكدُهُ : خبره.

[٢] قوله : بحيث لا يكون ... الخ ؛ اعلم أن الخلوة لتحققها شروط ولصحتها شروط ، أما الثاني فقد مرَّ ذكرها في المتن ، وأما الأول فقد أشار الشارح له لبعضها : فمنها : أن يكونا في مكان واحد.
ومنها : أن يكون ذلك المكان مما يصلح للخلوة والوطء، فخرج المسجد والحمام والطريق العام ونحو ذلك.
ومنها : أن لا يكون معهما عاقل فإن كان فلا خلوة.
ومنها : أن يكون الزوج عالماً بأنها زوجته فإن لم يعلم فلا خلوة.
(۳) قوله : عاقل ؛ احتراز عن غير العاقل كالمجنون، فإن وجوده ليس مانعاً عن تحقق الخلوة، وكذا وجود غير إنسان، وفي إطلاقه إشارة إلى أن وجود الصبي الذي يعقل مانع من تحقق الخلوة لا وجود الصبي الذي لا يعقل. [٤]قوله : كخلوة مجبوب ؛ المجبوبُ : هو الذي قُطِعَ ذَكَرُهُ وخصيتاه ، وقيل : قطعُ الخصيتين ليس بشرط في المحبوب. والخصي: على وزن فعيل مَن سُلَّت خصيتاه وبقي ذَكَرُه.
والعنينُ : - بكسر العين المهملة، وتشديد النون الأولى المكسورة - : هو من لا يقدر على الوطء لكبر السن، أو المرض، أو لفقدان القوة خلقة مع سلامة الذكر والخصيتين.
The book of Nikah, chapter of Mahr. And seclusion without a barrier to intercourse, physically, or legally, or naturally: such as illness that prevents intercourse, fasting Ramadan, ihram for obligatory or supererogatory pilgrimage, menstruation and postpartum bleeding. (Seclusion without a barrier to intercourse physically, or legally, or naturally: such as illness that prevents intercourse): this is analogous to the physical barrier. (Fasting Ramadan and ihram for obligatory or supererogatory pilgrimage): this is analogous to the legal barrier. (Menstruation and postpartum bleeding): this is analogous to the natural barrier. And from Umar ibn al-Khattab: 'When the curtains are lowered, the dowry becomes obligatory.' Recorded by Malik in al-Muwatta, al-Bayhaqi, Abd al-Razzaq, and similarly al-Daraqutni from Ali and Abu Ubayd in the book of Nikah from the four caliphs. The reasoning behind it is that seclusion generally leads to intercourse, and the reality of intercourse is something often hidden, so the cause that leads to it is given its status, just as sleep is given the status of breaking wudu, and with that... when she secludes with him, she surrenders the compensation (mahr), since she removed the barriers to the extent of her capability, so the compensation becomes obligatory. Hence it is stipulated in affirmed seclusion that there be no barrier to intercourse, because seclusion with a barrier does not lead to intercourse, so it is not given its status. [1] His saying: 'physically': meaning the barrier to intercourse is either physical, legal, or natural: i.e., something that prevents intercourse by physical, legal, or natural requirement. [2] His saying: 'prevents intercourse': is an adjective for illness, and he restricted it because not every illness prevents intercourse, and he made it general to include the illness of the husband and wife, including that the woman has ratq (flesh adhering at the site of intercourse), or has qarn (a bone or gland preventing entry). From the physical barriers: the husband or wife being too small to have intercourse, and the presence of a third person with them – some have made this an example of natural. [3] His saying: 'this is analogous to the barrier': natural, because blood at the site of intercourse is repulsive to healthy human nature. (Footnote references). It affirms it, like the seclusion of a majbub (one whose penis and testicles are cut), or an 'innin (impotent), or a khasi (castrated). And it does not harm that the legal barrier exists in them. 'It affirms it': meaning it affirms the mahr, so 'khalwah' is the subject and 'tu'akkiduhu' is the predicate. Know that what is meant by seclusion is their coming together such that there is no rational person with them in a place where no one can see them without their permission, or no one can see them due to darkness, and the husband knows that she is his wife. Like seclusion of a majbub, 'innin, or khasi. [1] His saying: 'And it does not harm': is a rebuttal to what is said: 'Verily intercourse during menstruation and postpartum bleeding is legally prohibited, so it is an example of a legal barrier – how can it be made an example of natural?' The essence of the rebuttal is that there is no issue because something can be an example for both. [2] His saying: 'such that there is no...' etc.: Know that seclusion has conditions for its actual occurrence and conditions for its validity. The latter have been mentioned in the text, and for the former the commentator has indicated some: Among them: that they be in one place. Among them: that the place be suitable for seclusion and intercourse – thus the mosque, bathhouse, public road, etc. are excluded. Among them: that there be no rational person with them – if there is, then there is no seclusion. Among them: that the husband know she is his wife – if he does not know, then no seclusion. [3] His saying: 'rational person': excludes the non-rational like the insane, for their presence does not prevent seclusion, and similarly the presence of a non-human. Its absolute usage indicates that the presence of a rational child prevents seclusion, not the presence of a non-rational child. [4] His saying: 'like seclusion of a majbub': al-majbub is one whose penis and testicles are cut; it is said that cutting the testicles is not a condition. Al-khasi is on the pattern of fa'il: one whose testicles are removed but his penis remains. Al-'innin (impotent) is one who cannot have intercourse due to old age, illness, or lack of natural strength with intact penis and testicles.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah, explaining the ruling that valid seclusion (khilwah sahihah) obligates the full mahr (dower) even without actual intercourse. The author first presents the base text which states that seclusion without any barrier—whether physical (like illness preventing intercourse), legal (like Ramadan fasting or ihram), or natural (like menstruation)—necessitates the mahr. The commentary then provides the legal proof: the famous athar of Umar ibn al-Khattab, 'When the curtains are lowered, the dowry becomes obligatory,' which is also reported from other caliphs. The rational basis (wajh) is then explained: seclusion is the predominant means leading to intercourse, and since intercourse itself is private, the means takes its place—analogous to how sleep replaces the breaking of wudu. Therefore, the mahr becomes due as compensation for the wife's submission to the husband. The commentary then clarifies the types of barriers in detail: physical barriers include illness (of either spouse), anatomical issues like ratq or qarn in the woman, or the husband/wife being too small; legal barriers include fasting and ihram; natural barriers include menstruation and postpartum bleeding. It also addresses a subtle point: the presence of a legal barrier does not invalidate the seclusion if it is natural—for example, menstruation is both a legal prohibition and a natural repulsion, but it primarily serves as a natural barrier. The later part of the passage discusses the conditions for valid seclusion: the couple must be alone in a place suitable for intercourse, with no rational third person present, and the husband must know she is his wife. Even if the husband is majbub (castrated), 'innin (impotent), or khasi (without testicles), seclusion still affirms the mahr, though the endnote clarifies that impotence due to old age or illness counts as natural. The text emphasizes that these conditions ensure seclusion is truly sufficient to imply intercourse, and thus the full mahr is wajib (obligatory).

Key Terms
الخلوة (al-khalwah): The husband and wife being alone together in a private place without any barrier to intercourse.
المانع (al-mani'): Anything that prevents intercourse, classified into physical (hissi), legal (shar'i), or natural (tab'i).
المهر (al-mahr): The dower given to the wife, which becomes fully wajib upon valid seclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered a natural barrier (مانع طبعي) to intercourse?
A) Fasting Ramadan
B) Illness preventing intercourse
C) Menstruation
D) Ihram for Hajj

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, valid seclusion (khalwah sahihah) makes the full mahr wajib, even without intercourse, provided there is no barrier and the conditions of place, privacy, and knowledge are met.

Valid Seclusion (Khalwah): Conditions and Effect on Mahr
Defining valid khalwah, its conditions, and its effect on mahr confirmation with an intra-Hanafi dispute on the majbub.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of valid khalwah according to the Hanafi school and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding khalwah with a majbub (castrated) husband, including the operative ruling (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
Valid khalwah confirms full mahr because the wife submits to her utmost capacity—even if the husband cannot consummate.

In Hanafi fiqh, valid seclusion (khalwah sahihah) carries legal consequences, most notably the confirmation of the full mahr. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah defines what constitutes valid khalwah, lists its conditions, and addresses a nuanced dispute between Abu Hanifah and his two students regarding khalwah with a castrated husband (majbub). Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between the wife's obligation and the husband's capacity.

Guided Path
1
Define khalwah and list its conditions
Read the passage carefully to extract the definition: the couple being together in a place where no one can see them without permission or due to darkness, with no sane person present, and the husband knowing she is his wife. Identify the four conditions mentioned.
Focus on the conditions for actual occurrence, not validity.
2
Identify the legal effect of valid khalwah
The passage states that valid khalwah confirms (tukidduhu) the mahr. That is, the full dower becomes due and payable.
This is the central rule: submission to the utmost capacity triggers the obligation.
3
Analyze the ruling for disabled husbands
The text explicitly mentions khalwah with a majbub (castrated), khasi (emasculated), or 'innin (impotent due to age/illness). In all cases, khalwah is valid and confirms full mahr according to Abu Hanifah.
The wife's action (submission) is the basis, not the husband's ability.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ regarding the majbub. They argue that the majbub is even more incapable than a sick person, so khalwah should not confirm mahr. Note their proof.
This is a dispute within the Hanafi school; the 'illah (legal cause) is contested.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the 'illah
The passage cites al-Binaya, stating that the ruling is based on the soundness of the organ. Thus, the 'innin is distinguished (organ sound), but for the majbub, Abu Hanifah's view is preferred because the wife has fulfilled her obligation.
The operative ruling (mufta bihi) is that khalwah with a majbub does confirm full mahr.

(The seclusion) confirms it: i.e., it confirms the dower. So 'khalwah' is the subject, and 'tukidduhu' is its predicate. Know that what is meant by khalwah is their being together such that there is no sane person with them in a place where no one can see them without their permission, or no one can see them due to darkness, and the husband knows that she is his wife. Like the seclusion of a majbub (castrated), or an 'innin (impotent), or a khasi (emasculated). [1] His statement: 'and it does not matter...' is a rebuttal to what might be said: that intercourse during menstruation and postpartum bleeding is prohibited legally, so that is an example of a legal preventer, so how can it be made an example of a natural preventer? The summary of the rebuttal is that there is no problem because a thing can be an example for both. [2] His statement: 'such that there is not...' etc.: Know that khalwah has conditions for its actual occurrence and conditions for its validity. As for the second, they have been mentioned in the matn. As for the first, the commentator has indicated some: Among them: that they are in one place. And in its absolute use, there is an indication that the presence of a boy who possesses reason prevents the realization of khalwah, not the presence of a boy who does not possess reason. The khasi, on the pattern of fa'il, is one whose testicles have been removed but his penis remains. The 'innin (with a kasra on the 'ayn, and tashdid on the first nun which is kasra) is one who is unable to have intercourse due to old age, illness, or lack of natural strength while the penis and testicles are sound. So the seclusion of such persons is valid, and the full dower is obligatory by it, because what is due from her by the contract is submission to the utmost of her capacity, and she has done that. Regarding the majbub, there is a disagreement between the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) based on the fact that he is more incapable than the sick person, unlike the 'innin, because the ruling is dependent upon the soundness of the organ. As in al-Binaya.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the concept of valid khalwah (seclusion) and its effect on confirming the full mahr (dower). The commentator begins by noting that the matn states that khalwah confirms or emphasizes the mahr. He then defines khalwah: it occurs when the husband and wife are alone together in a place where no one can see them without permission, or due to darkness, and the husband knows that she is his wife. The commentator lists conditions for the actual occurrence of khalwah: they must be in one place; the place must be suitable for seclusion and intercourse (so a mosque, bathroom, or public road does not qualify); there must be no sane person with them (the presence of an insane person or a non-human does not prevent khalwah, but the presence of a discerning child does); and the husband must know she is his wife (otherwise no khalwah). The text then addresses the case of a husband with a physical disability: a majbub (whose penis and testicles are cut), a khasi (testicles removed but penis intact), or an 'innin (impotent due to age or illness). The ruling is that khalwah with any of these is valid and confirms the full mahr, because the wife has fulfilled her obligation to submit to the best of her ability. However, there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the majbub: the two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the majbub is even more incapable than a sick person, so khalwah with him should not confirm the mahr, unlike the 'innin where the organ is sound. The relied-upon position (according to al-Binaya) is that the ruling revolves around the soundness of the organ, so the 'innin is distinguished. The passage thus clarifies the conditions and exceptions of khalwah, emphasizing the wife's submission as the basis for the mahr confirmation.

Key Terms
خلوة (khalwah): Seclusion of a married couple alone together in a place suitable for intercourse with no impediment.
مجبوب (majbub): A man whose penis and testicles have been cut off.
عنين ('innin): A man who cannot have intercourse due to age, illness, or natural lack of strength, but his organ and testicles are sound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of husband's seclusion with his wife triggers a disagreement between Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn?
A) The 'innin (impotent due to age)
B) The majbub (castrated)
C) The khasi (emasculated)
D) The sick person

Key Takeaway
Valid khalwah confirms full mahr based on the wife's submission; the condition of the husband's ability only affects the debate regarding the majbub, but the operative ruling follows Abu Hanifah.

Hanafi Rulings on 'Iddah and Mut'ah: Categories and Evidence
Rules of 'iddah and mut'ah with categories and proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Hanafi categories of divorced women regarding mut'ah (consolation gift) and identify which category makes mut'ah obligatory, citing Quran 2:236 and 2:241.

Core Idea
Mut'ah is obligatory only for a woman divorced before consummation without dower; for others it is recommended or absent.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies when a divorced woman is entitled to a consolation gift (mut'ah) and when she must observe a waiting period ('iddah). The Hanafi school distinguishes four scenarios based on consummation and dower stipulation, deriving rulings from specific Quranic verses and the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on 'iddah
Note that 'iddah is wajib in all cases of khalwah (privacy) due to istihsan, not qiyas. The rationale: protecting the womb from lineage mixing.
This ruling is istihsani, not qiyasi, because the 'iddah is a right of Shari'ah and child.
2
List the four categories of divorced women for mut'ah
Name the four cases: (1) no consummation, no dower → mut'ah wajib; (2) no consummation, with dower → no mut'ah (half dower); (3) consummation, no dower → mut'ah mustahabb; (4) consummation, with dower → mut'ah mustahabb.
These categories cover all possibilities; case of invalid dower is subsumed under case (1).
3
Examine Quranic evidence for each category
Verse 2:236 directly obligates mut'ah for case (1). Verse 2:241 gives a general command for all divorced women, interpreted as recommendation (mustahabb) for cases (3) and (4), limited by the hadith of Ibn 'Umar for case (2).
The Hanafi usul principle: a general command ('amm) can be made non-obligatory by specific evidence (takhsis).
4
Understand the handling of an invalid dower
If a dower is stipulated but invalid (e.g., something not permissible), the stipulation is void, making the case equivalent to 'no dower' – thus mut'ah becomes wajib if no consummation.
This explains why only four categories are needed; the fifth is merged into the first.

The 'iddah is obligatory in all cases as a precaution, and the mut'ah is obligatory for a divorced woman who has not been touched (consummated) and for whom no dower was stipulated. 'The 'iddah is obligatory in all cases': meaning in all the types of khalwah mentioned, whether a hindrance like illness or the like exists or not. 'And the mut'ah is obligatory for a divorced woman who has not been touched and for whom no dower was stipulated.' (Then extended commentary on differences between obligatory and voluntary acts in prayer and fasting, not directly relevant). [1] His statement: 'and the 'iddah is obligatory': by analogy it should not be obligatory because no khalwah occurred, but its obligation is istihsani (based on juristic preference), because the 'iddah is a right of the Shari'ah and a right of the child; therefore they cannot drop it, so precaution is taken in making it obligatory. Thus it is made obligatory in the case of invalid khalwah and also in the presence of hindrances that suggest the womb may be occupied by his fluid, and the 'iddah was prescribed to determine the purity of the womb lest another's fluid mix with another's sowing. As for the dower, it is wealth, so no precaution is taken in making it obligatory; rather it is only owed in full, so it is owed upon complete and valid khalwah, not upon invalid one. [2] His statement: 'for a divorced woman' excludes a woman whose husband dies, for she has no mut'ah whether she was touched or not, because upon death the entire stipulated dower is owed if stipulated, and the dower of the like if no dower was stipulated, as detailed before. [3] His statement: 'who has not been touched' is a descriptive phrase for 'divorced woman', and the negation of touching includes both actual touching and constructive touching. [PAGE_129] The Book of Marriage, the Chapter of Dower. 'And it is recommended for others except for one who had a dower stipulated for her and was divorced before touching.' The divorced women are four: 1. A woman divorced without being touched and without a stipulated dower: mut'ah is obligatory for her. 2. A woman divorced without being touched, but a dower was stipulated for her: mut'ah is not recommended for her. 3. A woman divorced after being touched, without a stipulated dower. 4. A woman divorced after being touched, with a stipulated dower: for these two, mut'ah is recommended. [1] His statement: 'for others' means other than the divorced woman previously mentioned. [2] His statement: 'so mut'ah is obligatory for her' due to His saying: 'There is no sin upon you if you divorce women while not yet touched them nor specified for them a dower, but provide for them—upon the affluent its capability and upon the constrained its capability—a provision with goodness.' (Quran 2:236) [3] His statement: 'so mut'ah is not recommended for her' is supported by the saying of Ibn 'Umar: 'Every divorced woman has mut'ah except the one for whom a dower was stipulated and he has not consummated, for her is half the dower' reported by al-Shafi'i, al-Bayhaqi, and others. [4] His statement: 'mut'ah is recommended for them' due to His saying: 'And divorced women have a provision with goodness.' (Quran 2:241) Al-Shafi'i made mut'ah obligatory for every divorced woman except the one who was not touched and a dower was stipulated for her. So the summary is that according to him, mut'ah is obligatory for all except this one, whereas according to us, it is obligatory for one and recommended for others except this one. If you say: There is a fifth case: a woman divorced before consummation, and a dower was stipulated for her, but the stipulation is invalid because what was stipulated as dower is not valid as dower, such that upon invalidity, the dower of the like becomes obligatory upon death and consummation, and upon divorce before consummation, mut'ah becomes obligatory as mentioned. So why did the commentator not mention it and limit the cases to four? I say: It is included in the first case, because the absolute absence of dower stipulation includes what was stipulated with something invalid as dower, because stipulating what cannot be made a dower is like not stipulating, so it is as if he meant by his statement 'no dower was stipulated for her' includes both real and constructive absence of stipulation.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āyah is a commentary (sharḥ) on the rules of ‘iddah (waiting period) and mut‘ah (consolation gift) for divorced women. The author begins by stating the primary ruling: ‘iddah is obligatory in all cases of khalwah (privacy with a spouse or invalid marriage) as a precaution, and mut‘ah is obligatory for a divorced woman who was not touched and had no dower stipulated. The commentator then elaborates.

For ‘iddah: By strict analogy (qiyās), ‘iddah should not be obligatory when no valid khalwah occurred, but the ruling is based on istiḥsān (juristic preference) because ‘iddah protects the right of the Sharī‘ah and the right of the child. The Sharī‘ah prescribes ‘iddah to ensure the womb is free from pregnancy, to avoid mixing lineages. Therefore, caution is exercised, and ‘iddah becomes obligatory even in cases of invalid khalwah or when obstacles like illness exist. In contrast, the dower (mahr) is a financial obligation, so it only becomes fully due upon complete and valid khalwah; no caution is extended to invalid situations.

The passage then details the types of mut‘ah based on Quran 2:236 and 2:241. The Hanafi position distinguishes four categories of divorced women:
1. Divorced without touching and without stipulated dower: mut‘ah is obligatory (wājib).
2. Divorced without touching but with a stipulated dower: mut‘ah is not recommended (even recommended is absent).
3. Divorced after touching without stipulated dower: mut‘ah is recommended (mustaḥabb).
4. Divorced after touching with stipulated dower: mut‘ah is also recommended.
The commentator clarifies that for the first category, obligation is established by the Quranic verse 2:236. For the second category, the absence of recommendation is supported by a narration of Ibn ‘Umar, reported by al-Shafi‘i and al-Bayhaqi, stating that only half the dower is due, no mut‘ah. For the last two categories, verse 2:241 (general command of mut‘ah for all divorced women) provides the basis, but the Hanafi school interprets it as recommendation (mustaḥabb) not obligation, unlike the Shafi‘i school.

A question arises about a fifth case: a woman divorced before consummation with an invalid dower stipulation. The commentator explains that this case is included in the first category because an invalid dower stipulation is legally equivalent to having no stipulation at all (the stipulation of what cannot be dower is void, so it is as if no dower was named). Thus, only four essential categories cover all possibilities.

The passage also contrasts the Hanafi and Shafi‘i positions on mut‘ah: Shafi‘i obligates mut‘ah for every divorced woman except the one with a stipulated dower and no consummation; Hanafi obligates it only for one and recommends for two others (excluding the second category). This ikhtilāf demonstrates the different weights given to the general command in verse 2:241 and the specific exception from the Sunnah.

Key Terms
العدة (al-‘iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband to ensure the womb is free from pregnancy.
المتعة (al-mut‘ah): A consolatory gift given to a divorced woman as a provision. In this passage, its ruling varies based on circumstances.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that departs from strict analogy (qiyās) in favor of a ruling that better serves the objectives of the Sharī‘ah.
الخلوة (al-khalwah): Valid privacy between a married couple that permits consummation; in Hanafi fiqh, it is treated as constructive touching for some rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in which case is mut'ah (consolatory gift) obligatory for a divorced woman?
A) She was touched and had a stipulated dower.
B) She was not touched and had no stipulated dower.
C) She was not touched and had a stipulated dower.
D) She was touched and had no stipulated dower.

Key Takeaway
Mut'ah is only obligatory for a divorced woman who was not touched and had no dower; the ruling shifts from obligation to recommendation or exclusion based on the presence of dower or consummation.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i on the Obligation of Mut'ah: Takhsis al-'Amm
Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on obligation of mut'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on mut'ah obligation by identifying their different treatments of Quran 2:241 and the hadith of Ibn 'Umar, and apply the concept of takhsis al-'amm.

Core Idea
Hanafis use takhsis (specification) to make the general command of mut'ah recommendatory, while Shafi'is keep it obligatory except where specified.

This passage highlights a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the obligation of mut'ah (consolation gift) for divorced women. At the heart of the dispute lies the interpretation of Quran 2:241 ('And divorced women have a provision') and the hadeeth of Ibn 'Umar. The Hanafi school uses the principle of takhṣīṣ al-‘āmm (specification of the general) to restrict the obligation to one category, while the Shafi'is uphold the general command as obligatory except for the one exception explicitly stated in the hadeeth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
The dispute is whether mut'ah is obligatory (wajib) or recommended (mustahabb) for a divorced woman who was touched (consummated) with or without dower, and for the untouched woman with dower (already agreed as no mut'ah).
Both schools agree mut'ah is wajib for the untouched woman without dower (via Quran 2:236) and not prescribed for the untouched woman with dower (via hadith).
2
State the Hanafi position and proof
Hanafis: Mut'ah is mustahabb for all touched women and for untouched without dower (the latter is wajib by 2:236). Their proof: Quran 2:241 is general, but it is made specific (makhsus) by the hadith of Ibn 'Umar which restricts obligation; the specific command in 2:236 is the only source of obligation.
Hanafis prioritize the specific (2:236) over the general (2:241) using the principle of takhsis.
3
State the Shafi'i position and proof
Shafi'is: Mut'ah is wajib for all divorced women except the untouched woman with dower (who gets half dower). Their proof: Quran 2:241 is a general command that creates obligation; the hadith of Ibn 'Umar confirms the exception, but does not lift obligation from others.
Shafi'is treat the general command as binding unless explicitly excluded, and the hadith only excludes one category.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond: If 2:241 were obligatory on all, then the exclusion of the woman with dower would be meaningless because she is not included in the obligation anyway (since she receives half dower). The specific command in 2:236 indicates that obligation is limited to that case; otherwise, why mention it separately? Also, the Companions did not universally oblige mut'ah.
The Hanafi argument relies on the idea that the general is not intended to create obligation when a specific instance is separately legislated.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In practice, under Shafi'i, a woman divorced after consummation without dower can demand mut'ah as a right; under Hanafi, she cannot enforce it, but it is recommended (the husband is encouraged to give).
The difference is in the legal enforceability of the gift.

[٢] قوله : لمطلقة ؛ احتراز عن المتوفى عنها زوجها ، فإنه لا متعة لها موطوءة كانت أو غير موطوءة، فإن بالموت يجبُ كلُّ المهر المسمى إن كان، ومهر المثل إن لم يكن سَمَّى المهر كما مر تفصيله.
[٣] قوله : لم توطأ ؛ الجملة صفة لمطلقة، والمراد بالوطء المنفي أعم من الوطء حقيقة، ومن الوطء دلالة.

أخرجه
[٤] قوله : تستحب لهما المتعة ؛ لقوله : وَالْمُطَلَّقَاتِ مَتَاعُ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ (۳) ، وأوجب الشافِعِيُّ : المتعة لكلِّ مطلقة إلا التي لم توطأ، وقد سمى لها، فالحاصل أن عنده تجب لكلّ مطلقة إلا هذه، وعندنا تجب لواحدة وتستحب لغيرها إلا هذه. فإن قلت : هنا صورة خامسة، وهي أن تكون مطلقة قبل الدخول، وقد سُمِّي لها مهر، لكن فسدت التسمية بأن سُمِّي في المهر ما لا يصلح مهراً، فإن عند فسادها يجب مهر المثل بالموت وبالوطء، وبالطلاق قبل الوطء تجب المتعة كما مرَّ ذكره ، فَلِمَ لم يذكره الشارح ، وحصر الصور في أربع ؟
قلت : هي مندرجة في الصورة الأولى، فإنَّ عدم تسمية المهر مطلقاً يلحقه ما سمى فيه ما لا يصلح مهراً ، فإنَّ تسمية ما لا يمكن جعله مهراً : كعدم التسمية، فكأنه أراد بقوله : لم يسم لها مهراً أعم من أن يكونَ عدم التسمية حقيقة أو حكماً، ثم في
The 'iddah is obligatory in all cases as a precaution, and the mut'ah is obligatory for a divorced woman who has not been touched (consummated) and for whom no dower was stipulated. 'The 'iddah is obligatory in all cases': meaning in all the types of khalwah mentioned, whether a hindrance like illness or the like exists or not. 'And the mut'ah is obligatory for a divorced woman who has not been touched and for whom no dower was stipulated.' (Then extended commentary on differences between obligatory and voluntary acts in prayer and fasting, not directly relevant). [1] His statement: 'and the 'iddah is obligatory': by analogy it should not be obligatory because no khalwah occurred, but its obligation is istihsani (based on juristic preference), because the 'iddah is a right of the Shari'ah and a right of the child; therefore they cannot drop it, so precaution is taken in making it obligatory. Thus it is made obligatory in the case of invalid khalwah and also in the presence of hindrances that suggest the womb may be occupied by his fluid, and the 'iddah was prescribed to determine the purity of the womb lest another's fluid mix with another's sowing. As for the dower, it is wealth, so no precaution is taken in making it obligatory; rather it is only owed in full, so it is owed upon complete and valid khalwah, not upon invalid one. [2] His statement: 'for a divorced woman' excludes a woman whose husband dies, for she has no mut'ah whether she was touched or not, because upon death the entire stipulated dower is owed if stipulated, and the dower of the like if no dower was stipulated, as detailed before. [3] His statement: 'who has not been touched' is a descriptive phrase for 'divorced woman', and the negation of touching includes both actual touching and constructive touching. [PAGE_129] The Book of Marriage, the Chapter of Dower. 'And it is recommended for others except for one who had a dower stipulated for her and was divorced before touching.' The divorced women are four: 1. A woman divorced without being touched and without a stipulated dower: mut'ah is obligatory for her. 2. A woman divorced without being touched, but a dower was stipulated for her: mut'ah is not recommended for her. 3. A woman divorced after being touched, without a stipulated dower. 4. A woman divorced after being touched, with a stipulated dower: for these two, mut'ah is recommended. [1] His statement: 'for others' means other than the divorced woman previously mentioned. [2] His statement: 'so mut'ah is obligatory for her' due to His saying: 'There is no sin upon you if you divorce women while not yet touched them nor specified for them a dower, but provide for them—upon the affluent its capability and upon the constrained its capability—a provision with goodness.' (Quran 2:236) [3] His statement: 'so mut'ah is not recommended for her' is supported by the saying of Ibn 'Umar: 'Every divorced woman has mut'ah except the one for whom a dower was stipulated and he has not consummated, for her is half the dower' reported by al-Shafi'i, al-Bayhaqi, and others. [4] His statement: 'mut'ah is recommended for them' due to His saying: 'And divorced women have a provision with goodness.' (Quran 2:241) Al-Shafi'i made mut'ah obligatory for every divorced woman except the one who was not touched and a dower was stipulated for her. So the summary is that according to him, mut'ah is obligatory for all except this one, whereas according to us, it is obligatory for one and recommended for others except this one. If you say: There is a fifth case: a woman divorced before consummation, and a dower was stipulated for her, but the stipulation is invalid because what was stipulated as dower is not valid as dower, such that upon invalidity, the dower of the like becomes obligatory upon death and consummation, and upon divorce before consummation, mut'ah becomes obligatory as mentioned. So why did the commentator not mention it and limit the cases to four? I say: It is included in the first case, because the absolute absence of dower stipulation includes what was stipulated with something invalid as dower, because stipulating what cannot be made a dower is like not stipulating, so it is as if he meant by his statement 'no dower was stipulated for her' includes both real and constructive absence of stipulation.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āyah is a commentary (sharḥ) on the rules of ‘iddah (waiting period) and mut‘ah (consolation gift) for divorced women. The author begins by stating the primary ruling: ‘iddah is obligatory in all cases of khalwah (privacy with a spouse or invalid marriage) as a precaution, and mut‘ah is obligatory for a divorced woman who was not touched and had no dower stipulated. The commentator then elaborates.

For ‘iddah: By strict analogy (qiyās), ‘iddah should not be obligatory when no valid khalwah occurred, but the ruling is based on istiḥsān (juristic preference) because ‘iddah protects the right of the Sharī‘ah and the right of the child. The Sharī‘ah prescribes ‘iddah to ensure the womb is free from pregnancy, to avoid mixing lineages. Therefore, caution is exercised, and ‘iddah becomes obligatory even in cases of invalid khalwah or when obstacles like illness exist. In contrast, the dower (mahr) is a financial obligation, so it only becomes fully due upon complete and valid khalwah; no caution is extended to invalid situations.

The passage then details the types of mut‘ah based on Quran 2:236 and 2:241. The Hanafi position distinguishes four categories of divorced women:
1. Divorced without touching and without stipulated dower: mut‘ah is obligatory (wājib).
2. Divorced without touching but with a stipulated dower: mut‘ah is not recommended (even recommended is absent).
3. Divorced after touching without stipulated dower: mut‘ah is recommended (mustaḥabb).
4. Divorced after touching with stipulated dower: mut‘ah is also recommended.
The commentator clarifies that for the first category, obligation is established by the Quranic verse 2:236. For the second category, the absence of recommendation is supported by a narration of Ibn ‘Umar, reported by al-Shafi‘i and al-Bayhaqi, stating that only half the dower is due, no mut‘ah. For the last two categories, verse 2:241 (general command of mut‘ah for all divorced women) provides the basis, but the Hanafi school interprets it as recommendation (mustaḥabb) not obligation, unlike the Shafi‘i school.

A question arises about a fifth case: a woman divorced before consummation with an invalid dower stipulation. The commentator explains that this case is included in the first category because an invalid dower stipulation is legally equivalent to having no stipulation at all (the stipulation of what cannot be dower is void, so it is as if no dower was named). Thus, only four essential categories cover all possibilities.

The passage also contrasts the Hanafi and Shafi‘i positions on mut‘ah: Shafi‘i obligates mut‘ah for every divorced woman except the one with a stipulated dower and no consummation; Hanafi obligates it only for one and recommends for two others (excluding the second category). This ikhtilāf demonstrates the different weights given to the general command in verse 2:241 and the specific exception from the Sunnah.

Key Terms
تخصيص العام (takhṣīṣ al-‘āmm): The specification of a general text, limiting its application to some instances while excluding others based on evidence (like a hadith or another verse).
المتعة (al-mut‘ah): A consolatory gift to a divorced woman; its obligation is disputed.
الخلاف (al-ikhtilāf): Scholarly disagreement; in fiqh, differences of opinion among mujtahids.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on mut'ah for a woman divorced after consummation without a dower?
A) Hanafi: mustahabb, Shafi'i: mustahabb.
B) Hanafi: wajib, Shafi'i: wajib.
C) Hanafi: mustahabb (not enforceable), Shafi'i: wajib (enforceable).
D) Hanafi: wajib, Shafi'i: not prescribed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute over mut'ah illustrates the usul principle of takhsis al-'amm, where a general Quranic command is limited by a specific Sunnah, leading to different rulings on enforceability.

The Four Categories of Divorced Women Regarding Mut'ah
Classification of divorced women for mut'ah rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify divorced women into four categories and state the Hanafi ruling on mut'ah for each, distinguishing between obligatory (wajib) and recommended (mustahabb).

Core Idea
Mut'ah is wajib only for one type, mustahabb for two, and not recommended for the fourth.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi classification of divorced women concerning mut'ah. You will learn the four categories and the legal ruling (wajib, mustahabb, or neither) for each. We will also see the proof texts and the dispute with al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
List the four categories
Identify the four types of divorced women based on two variables: consummation (before or after) and dower specification (specified or not).
2
Identify the ruling for each
State whether mut'ah is wajib, mustahabb, or neither for each category according to the Hanafi school.
Type 1: wajib; Type 2: neither; Type 3: mustahabb; Type 4: mustahabb.
3
Locate the Quranic evidence
Find the Quranic verses cited: 2:236 for type 1 (obligation) and 2:241 for types 3 and 4 (recommendation). Understand how the imperative vs. general statement yields different hukm levels.
4
Note the hadith of Ibn 'Umar
Understand the hadith: 'Every divorced woman has mut'ah except the one for whom a dower was specified and he did not enter.' This supports the Hanafi exclusion of type 2.
Reported by al-Shafi'i and al-Bayhaqi.
5
Compare with al-Shafi'i's position
Analyze the dispute: al-Shafi'i makes mut'ah wajib for all except type 2, while Hanafis limit obligation to type 1. Examine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
Hanafi tarjih: the hadith of Ibn 'Umar and the contextual interpretation of the verses.

Book of Nikah, Chapter of Mahr. Divorced women are four: 1. A woman divorced before consummation and no dower was specified for her; then mut'ah is obligatory for her. 2. A woman divorced before consummation and a dower was specified for her; she is the one for whom mut'ah is not recommended. 3. A woman divorced after consummation and no dower was specified for her. 4. A woman divorced after consummation and she has a dower; for these two, mut'ah is recommended for them. [1] His statement: 'except for other than her'; meaning except the divorced woman previously mentioned. [2] His statement: 'then mut'ah is obligatory for her'; due to His statement: 'There is no sin upon you if you divorce women while you have not yet touched them nor specified for them an obligation. But provide for them, on the wealthy a measure and on the poor a measure, with kindness.' [3] His statement: 'She is the one for whom mut'ah is not recommended'; that is indicated by the saying of Ibn 'Umar: 'Every divorced woman has mut'ah except the one for whom a dower was specified and he did not enter upon her; then her due is half the dower.' Reported by al-Shafi'i and al-Bayhaqi and others. [4] His statement: 'Mut'ah is recommended for both of them'; due to His statement: 'And for divorced women, provision with kindness.' And al-Shafi'i made mut'ah obligatory for every divorced woman except the one who was not touched and a dower was specified for her. So the result is that in his view it is obligatory for every divorced woman except this one, and in our view it is obligatory for one and recommended for others except this one. If you say: Here is a fifth case: a woman divorced before consummation and a dower was specified for her, but the specification became invalid because something that is not valid as a dower was specified; then upon invalidation, the dower of the like becomes obligatory upon death and upon intercourse, and upon divorce before intercourse mut'ah becomes obligatory as previously mentioned. So why did the commentator not mention it and limit the cases to four? I say: It is included in the first case, because the non-specification of the dower absolutely is joined by what is specified that is not valid as a dower, for specifying what cannot be made a dower is like non-specification. So it is as if he intended by his statement 'no dower was specified for her' to be general, whether the non-specification is literal or constructive. Then in the case of invalid specification, mut'ah is only obligatory if the invalidation is from every aspect. Otherwise, if it is valid from one aspect and not from another, mut'ah is not obligatory, even if the dower of the like is obligatory upon entry. As if he married her for one thousand dirhams and her honor, or for one thousand and that he would give her a gift; then if he divorces her before consummation, she gets half the thousand, not mut'ah, even though if he had entered upon her, the dower of the like would be obligatory, not less than the thousand, because the specified dower is not invalid from every aspect, because upon the assumption of her honor and the gift, the thousand is obligatory, not the dower of the like. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. [1] His statement: 'The result'; meaning the result of the rulings of the four cases: that the recommendation of mut'ah is specific to the case of being touched, whether the dower is specified or not. If the dower is specified, its full amount is obligatory; if not specified, the dower of the like is obligatory; and mut'ah is recommended in both cases. And the obligation of mut'ah is specific to the case of no intercourse and no specification, and the non-recommendation and non-obligation is specific to the case of existence of specification and no intercourse. [2] His statement: 'Because... etc.'; an explanation of the rationale of the legislation of mut'ah: that when he divorced her after taking the benefits of her sexual organ, he brought sadness upon her by separation, so he should give her something extra beyond the obligatory amount to bring happiness and joy to her and to compensate for what she missed. [3] His statement: 'she handed over'; meaning after she handed over the subject of the contract, which is the benefits of the sexual organ. [4] His statement: 'which is'; meaning the obligatory is giving the determined dower in the case of it being determined, and the dower of the like in the case of it not being determined.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of divorced women concerning the ruling of mut'ah (consolatory gift). The author first enumerates four categories, then provides scriptural proofs, a comparative discussion with al-Shafi'i, and addresses a potential fifth case. The Hanafi position is that mut'ah is obligatory only for a woman divorced before consummation with no dower specified (type 1). It is recommended (mustahabb) for types 3 and 4 (those divorced after consummation, whether dower was specified or not). It is neither obligatory nor recommended for type 2 (divorced before consummation with a specified dower). The commentator includes a hadith from Ibn 'Umar supporting this and cites Quranic verses (2:236 and 2:241). Al-Shafi'i differs, making mut'ah obligatory for all divorced women except type 2; the Hanafi response is that type 1 is obligatory by specific verse and the rest are covered by the general verse which implies recommendation. A possible fifth case—invalid specification of dower before consummation—is subsumed under type 1 if the invalidation is total; if partial, it follows a different ruling. The commentator explains the rationale: mut'ah compensates for the sadness of divorce after intimacy.

Key Terms
المتعة (al-mut'ah): A consolatory gift given to a divorced woman, consisting of money or goods, as compensation for the termination of marriage.
المهر (al-mahr): The dower (bridal gift) specified in the marriage contract, which becomes the wife's property.
فساد التسمية (fasad al-tasmiyah): The invalidity of the specification of the dower, e.g., specifying something that is not legally acceptable as mahr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, for which type of divorced woman is mut'ah obligatory (wajib)?
A) A woman divorced after consummation with a specified dower.
B) A woman divorced before consummation with no dower specified.
C) A woman divorced after consummation with no dower specified.
D) A woman divorced before consummation with a specified dower.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes four categories of divorced women, making mut'ah wajib only for one, mustahabb for two, and not recommended for the fourth.
